<div id="review_901081572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/901081572" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/901081572?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007491452" href="/book/show/20754586-the-shock-of-the-fall">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer901081572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1567096018227480657">Matt Homes is a young man from Bristol with schizophrenia, writing out his life story which centres around the death of his Downs Syndrome brother when they were children. As Matt’s narrative progresses, we learn there’s more to his brother’s death than he initially lets on and that this is why he carries around feelings of guilt. <br/><br/>The Shock of the Fall is a character portrait rather than a narrative driven book - unfortunately, for a character piece, I never really felt like Matt had much of a w</span>
  <span id="freeText1567096018227480657" style="display:none">Matt Homes is a young man from Bristol with schizophrenia, writing out his life story which centres around the death of his Downs Syndrome brother when they were children. As Matt’s narrative progresses, we learn there’s more to his brother’s death than he initially lets on and that this is why he carries around feelings of guilt. <br /><br />The Shock of the Fall is a character portrait rather than a narrative driven book - unfortunately, for a character piece, I never really felt like Matt had much of a well-defined character to start with; it’s sketchy at best and oftentimes feels uneven. The best work Nathan Filer did with Matt was show how his brother’s death devastated his life but how much of that life was due to that haunting event or his own schizophrenia is unclear. <br /><br />I have no experience with schizophrenia so I can’t say whether Matt’s behaviour is realistic or not, or even when or how the illness manifests throughout the narrative, but I was never sure whether his actions were the result of personal trauma or mental illness. <br /><br />What I mean is, was the point of the novel about a person coming to terms with a traumatic childhood experience or about someone dealing with mental illness, and if so, why have these two unconnected elements side by side - what’s the reader supposed to focus on? I guess given the way the novel ended, it was about Matt coming to terms with his brother’s death, but what that has to do with his schizophrenia is unclear. Did he even need to have schizophrenia? Maybe the hallucinatory sequences wouldn’t have had as much weight if he did, but what a contrived reason to have that illness if that was the point! <br /><br />It’s an easy to follow narrative but a very dull one. Filer uses his own experience as a mental health nurse to inform the novel and the passages set in the treatment centre were convincing. If there’s one element that stays with you, it’s the clear picture of mental health treatment in England today, and how soul-draining it is for patients. <br /><br />But other than that, there’s no plot to follow, few other characters, and a narrator with very little personality or much to say. It’s an extremely slow read despite being relatively short and the “reveal” of his brother’s death happens about 60 pages before the end when it should’ve been the conclusion of the book - those last 60 pages were a real slog only to build up to an overly sentimental, corny Hollywood-esque ending. <br /><br />At best this reads like a Young Adult novel that’s trying a bit too hard to be literary or a creative writing assignment completely devoid of feeling or insight. After the novel is a brief interview with Filer and I noticed when he was asked “were you reading any books during the writing process” he mentions Catcher in the Rye and Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night Time, which is really telling. Parts of the book - like the typewriter font pages - read like Holden Caulfield’s voice, where Matt’s character suddenly becomes very biting and he turns into a whiny teenager, while other parts of the book are more matter-of-fact and observational as if Christopher Boone were speaking. There are even pointless doodles thrown in a la Curious Incident! <br /><br />The Shock of the Fall is a very shallow novel that wears its influences too obviously to be taken seriously as an original or thoughtful work. It tries some strange stylistic tricks like drawings or font changes to little effect and for no reason - variety maybe to distract the reader from the dull prose? - and rather than be moved by Matt’s story, I was frequently bored or unclear as to what the point of many scenes were. <br /><br />This book won the 2013 Costa Prize but I shouldn’t be surprised that this prize-winning novel is awful. I mean, who knows how to pick out great literature better than a judging panel made up of the lead singer of Texas, an actress who was once in a George Clooney movie, and a TV presenter, for a prize sponsored by a coffee chain? </span>
  <a data-text-id="1567096018227480657" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_666436983" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/666436983" />
    <a title="Raven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5973984-raven"><img alt="Raven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338499933p2/5973984.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/666436983?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raven" href="/user/show/5973984-raven">Raven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer666436983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6339832825463761627">It is a rare thing to find a novel addressing the issues of mental illness handled in such a deft and compelling manner, but this is exactly what Filer has achieved in this accomplished debut novel. Drawing on Filer's own experiences of working within this field of mental health, there is an authenticity to the book that further compounds the effect and enjoyment of this, at times, heartwrenching story. Narrated by Matthew, a young adult, in the grip of schizophrenia, whose life has been hugely </span>
  <span id="freeText6339832825463761627" style="display:none">It is a rare thing to find a novel addressing the issues of mental illness handled in such a deft and compelling manner, but this is exactly what Filer has achieved in this accomplished debut novel. Drawing on Filer's own experiences of working within this field of mental health, there is an authenticity to the book that further compounds the effect and enjoyment of this, at times, heartwrenching story. Narrated by Matthew, a young adult, in the grip of schizophrenia, whose life has been hugely impacted on by the death of his brother Simon some years previously, Filer captures the true voice of and frustrations that Matthew experiences as his illness waxes and wanes throughout the book. The effects of the rise and fall of his symptons are cleverly illustrated by the clever incorporation of different typesets and fonts, which further brings home to the reader the essential aspects of Matthew's day-to-day- struggles with his mental health. Matthew's narrative is utterly compelling, gravitating between anger, despair and moments of humour, that challenges the reader and our perceptions of him as a character. Filer also stresses the impact of Matthew's schizophrenia on Matthew's family in a number of carefully wrought tableaus that really bring home the darkness of mental illness on the perceived normality of family life. The novel never descends into the trap of sentimentality, nor is it a completely depressing and mawkish study of life in the shadow of schizophrenia. It is an exceptionally balanced mix of truth, poignancy and, in some ways hope, that adds to its overall strength as a remarkable debut that deserves to be read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6339832825463761627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_758280834" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/758280834" />
    <a title="Jessica Jeffers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/188447-jessica-jeffers"><img alt="Jessica Jeffers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459125628p2/188447.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/758280834?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica Jeffers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Jeffers" href="/user/show/188447-jessica-jeffers">Jessica Jeffers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781250026989" href="/book/show/17286816-where-the-moon-isn-t">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/188447-jessica-jeffers?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer758280834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8425241123754312747">Maybe it's because of my profession -- I work in marketing for a scholarly publisher -- but this book could serve as an interesting study in how book marketing works in the trade publishing world. I spent a lot of time looking at how the original British title -- <i>The Shock of the Fall</i>, a line repeated throughout the book -- and the original British descriptive copy became Americanized. The copy that accompanies the British edition (on GR at least, which tends to be fairly faithful to jacket copy</span>
  <span id="freeText8425241123754312747" style="display:none">Maybe it's because of my profession -- I work in marketing for a scholarly publisher -- but this book could serve as an interesting study in how book marketing works in the trade publishing world. I spent a lot of time looking at how the original British title -- <i>The Shock of the Fall</i>, a line repeated throughout the book -- and the original British descriptive copy became Americanized. The copy that accompanies the British edition (on GR at least, which tends to be fairly faithful to jacket copy) is vague and doesn't really give you much in terms of what the plot actually is: <blockquote>There are books you can’t stop reading, which keep you up all night. There are books which let us into the hidden parts of life and make them vividly real. There are books which, because of the sheer skill with which every word is chosen, linger in your mind for days. <i>The Shock of the Fall</i> is all of these books. <p><i>The Shock of the Fall</i> is an extraordinary portrait of one man’s descent into mental illness. It is a brave and groundbreaking novel from one of the most exciting new voices in fiction.</p></blockquote>The American copy, I found, was kind of misleading:<blockquote>While on vacation with their parents, Matthew Homes and his older brother snuck out in the middle of the night. Only Matthew came home safely. Ten years later, Matthew tells us, he has found a way to bring his brother back...<p>What begins as the story of a lost boy turns into a story of a brave man yearning to understand what happened that night, in the years since, and to his very person. Unafraid to look at the shadows of our hearts, Nathan Filer's rare and brilliant debut <i>Where the Moon Isn't</i> shows us the strength that is rooted in resilience and love.</p></blockquote>The fact that the American marketing strategy downplays the mental illness side of this story is a shame because this book is a brilliant depiction of psychological descent and the ways in which the system can fail the mentally ill. <br /><br />The book is narrated by nineteen-year-old Matthew, a schizophrenic who has spent some time in a residential facility and bounces around an outpatient setting. Matthew began writing his story when his grandmother gave him an old typewriter as a gift. The narration weaves back and forth between relative present day and and ten years ago, when Matthew's brother Simon died. It can sometimes be a little hard to follow because Matthew is not the most reliable narrator – you sometimes have to figure out for yourself when a particular chapter takes place in the overall timeline of the story, especially the earlier chapters. Matthew circles back around to specific ideas again and again, and he consistently breaks the literary fourth wall by addressing what his reader might or might not know about the story or the setting or the characters coming into the book. It’s a really interesting exploration of the unreliable narrator and really drives home the lack of psychological clarity. <br /><br />I feel like a lot of readers might be disappointed by this book because it very much is not what is described in the jacket copy for the US edition. This isn't a missing child book or a mystery in the sense that I normally think of those books -- even the more literary ones. There's not really a lot of suspense, especially since the jacket copy reveals plot point from the last fifty pages. Honestly? This read a little like <i>The Bell Jar</i> updated for the 21st century, with a male schizophrenic narrator instead of a depressed female. I very much enjoyed it, but it definitely was not what I expected. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8425241123754312747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1007436230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1007436230?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ikram" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ikram" href="/user/show/17065991-ikram">Ikram</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007491452" href="/book/show/20754586-the-shock-of-the-fall">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17065991-ikram?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17065991-ikram?shelf=smells-like-pain">smells-like-pain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17065991-ikram?shelf=made-me-cry">made-me-cry</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1007436230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7905151228096837398"><b>"There was the shock of the fall and the blood on my knee and Simon had carried me. He carried me all the way to safety, all by himself, because he loved me."</b><br/><br/>This book left me speechless. It was so honest and gave a very real insight to both death and mental illness. The first thing that came to my mind when I started reading this book was that it somehow reminded me of The Catcher in the Rye by J D Salinger. I'm not sure why. <br/><br/>After reading this blurb, I picked up the book immediately:<br/><b>"I’ll tell</b></span>
  <span id="freeText7905151228096837398" style="display:none"><b>"There was the shock of the fall and the blood on my knee and Simon had carried me. He carried me all the way to safety, all by himself, because he loved me."</b><br><br>This book left me speechless. It was so honest and gave a very real insight to both death and mental illness. The first thing that came to my mind when I started reading this book was that it somehow reminded me of The Catcher in the Rye by J D Salinger. I'm not sure why. <br><br>After reading this blurb, I picked up the book immediately:<br><b>"I’ll tell you what happened because it will be a good way to introduce my brother. His name’s Simon. I think you’re going to like him. I really do. But in a couple of pages he’ll be dead. And he was never the same after that."</b> <br><br>The shock of the fall is the story of Matthew Homes, narrated by himself. Matthew suffers from a mental illness and tries to deal with the death of his older brother Simon, which is basically the point where everything starts going downhill for Matt. He used to be a little jealous of his brother because the latter was a child with special needs so he always got all the attention of their parents, but when Simon dies tragically, he continues to live in Matt's mind. <br><br>As Matthews mental health deteriorates, his narration becomes less coherent. It is almost impossible to decide which parts of the novel actually happened and which ones were just the creation of his own mind. Matt is not the most reliable narrator and sometimes he doesn't even make sense. He jumps from one topic to another and that's when the line between the past memories, the present consciousness and the future expectations blurs. <br><br>Matt's mental illness was not taken lightly in this book, and this is what made it feel so realistic in my opinion. It wasn't introduced as something that can be fixed overnight. It's a daily struggle for the patient and his family.<br><br>The only thing I didn't like about this book is that it made me feel so sad but that's not enough to take a star off. <br><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1386424972ra/7361901.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br><br><br>. </span>

<div id="review_1393366840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1393366840" />
    <a title="Emmanuel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26047226-emmanuel"><img alt="Emmanuel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1440458237p2/26047226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1393366840?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emmanuel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emmanuel" href="/user/show/26047226-emmanuel">Emmanuel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007491452" href="/book/show/20754586-the-shock-of-the-fall">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1393366840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13469903466227699468"><b>*4.5 stars</b><br/>The Shock of the Fall is the story of a boy desperately clinging to the memory of his brother; out of guilt, out of fear. It's such a hauntingly realistic depiction of mental illness, that it becomes difficult to continue reading, because from a certain point onward, every page breaks away a little piece of your heart.<br/><br/><b>"Spring sunshine painted pillars of white across my carpet"</b><br/>Filer's writing is the backbone of the novel. Don't get me wrong, it's not the only good part of the book, but </span>
  <span id="freeText13469903466227699468" style="display:none"><b>*4.5 stars</b><br />The Shock of the Fall is the story of a boy desperately clinging to the memory of his brother; out of guilt, out of fear. It's such a hauntingly realistic depiction of mental illness, that it becomes difficult to continue reading, because from a certain point onward, every page breaks away a little piece of your heart.<br /><br /><b>"Spring sunshine painted pillars of white across my carpet"</b><br />Filer's writing is the backbone of the novel. Don't get me wrong, it's not the only good part of the book, but in my opinion, it elevates the story to a whole new level. At times I felt I was reading without having to read, felt like he was telling me the story in person and it wasn't just words on paper. Even from the very first page this was apparent, because this novel has one of the greatest first paragraphs I've ever read. <br /><br />This book brought me right back to the ground. <br />Reading is, a lot of the time, just another form of entertainment; something we use to brighten our days because the stories are fun and the possibilities endless, but this definitely isn't one of those novels. It's soothing, it's strange but it's oh so real. It deals with several serious themes such as mental illness, the passing of life, and human nature, and deals with them so well too, suggesting new perspectives and giving useful insight. <br /><br />In the book, and this isn't a spoiler don't worry, Matt tells us that he has trouble separating dreams from reality. And now having finished the book, I know exactly what Matt means because that's exactly how I felt about this book. The jumping forward and back in time without notice gives a hazy, dream-like quality that I enjoyed for the most part, but at times it did feel unnecessary and did detract from my enjoyment of a certain part of the book.<br /><br />Matt was an incredible character to read about, but he did make me feel quite frustrated. Frustrated because I wanted him to get better so badly and he didn't seem to even want that for himself. But that just gives us more insight into how families like this must feel; how difficult it must be, and yet they still do it. It was a great portrayal of the love family members have for each other.<br /><br />Again to re-iterate, this was great novel; haunting and heart-breaking and real. Would recommend to everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13469903466227699468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_974816189" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/974816189" />
    <a title="Leah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15797955-leah"><img alt="Leah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422306086p2/15797955.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/974816189?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah" href="/user/show/15797955-leah">Leah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer974816189" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6388925248789018709">For a debut novel, <b>
  <i>The Shock of the Fall</i>
</b> was freaking awesome. Hell, for a <i>however many numbered novel</i>, this book was pretty spectacular.<br/><br/>This book follows Matthew and adult mental illness and very real family connections. It shows how mental illness can affect everyone's lives and how the person with the illness sees themselves, their family, and life/society. It was raw and bitter and beautiful to read.<br/><br/>Filer did just a fantastic job at giving Matthew an original and very genuine voice. I ma</span>
  <span id="freeText6388925248789018709" style="display:none">For a debut novel, <b>
  <i>The Shock of the Fall</i>
</b> was freaking awesome. Hell, for a <i>however many numbered novel</i>, this book was pretty spectacular.<br /><br />This book follows Matthew and adult mental illness and very real family connections. It shows how mental illness can affect everyone's lives and how the person with the illness sees themselves, their family, and life/society. It was raw and bitter and beautiful to read.<br /><br />Filer did just a fantastic job at giving Matthew an original and very genuine voice. I may not have liked him some of the times (partly because he's a bit of an asshole), but I connected with Matthew pretty early on. His struggles with himself, his family, and his illness were heart wrenching to witness. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>The Shock of the Fall</i>
</b> is told entirely in Matthew's point of view, and it is written/told in a diary-type format (without the weird dated headers - which I really liked <i>not</i> seeing). Filer did something pretty neat by changing up the text from traditional font to typewritten. It means something in the story and being able to see the changes really helped elevate this story to a whole new level for me.<br /><br />What kept this novel from being 5 stars was the slow/disjointed beginning. It took me a while to get used to the writing and because I couldn't grasp the changes quick enough, my reading of this book for about the first 50 pages or so was just as disjointed and slow as reading the novel felt to me.<br /><br />Overall, I really enjoyed this book. There was a bit of hype around <b>
  <i>The Shock of the Fall</i>
</b> and sometimes the hype isn't deserved. For this novel though, it really was. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6388925248789018709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('O1+5Gb0JE579zljS+4g8CDI1F89msJhB8tNUwIIL+ShJm0MFnt1v7E5Q4Tggq811aKbqBUopA1BYlHwWhRDtGg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YqsDw48csLMR5slj/TQhzXVysy3a68f1S8FQu6MBs0IQb/nfrMjMwaJ4cIkmF9CwL+FO5/ZyXOThhnhtpBqncA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6569002.Nathan_Filer">About Nathan Filer</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6569002.Nathan_Filer"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1560195415p3/6569002.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6569002.Nathan_Filer">Nathan Filer
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
483
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12025815782066530108">Nathan Filer is a writer and lecturer in creative writing. His stand-up poetry has been a regular fixture at festivals and spoken-word events across the UK and has been broadcast on BBC 3 television and radio 4, 7, and 5 Live. He is also a BBC Best New Filmmaker and holds an MA in creative writing from Bath Spa University. He lives in Bristol with his partner and their daughter.</span>

</div>
</div>
